10.1016/j.jmp.2019.102296
Flexibility of roles is a salient characteristic of Bayesian statistical models when compared with models of classical statistics. For example, a quantity that at one stage of a Bayesian construction plays the role of conditioning variable may at a later stage play the role of conditioned variable, and vice versa. This characteristic is strictly related to the property that in a Bayesian model it is not only the empirically measurable quantities and the statistics computable on them, but also the parameters and possible hyper-parameters, that are endowed with probability distributions, so that there is a common formal language – the language of probability theory – that facilitates the exchanges between the domain of empirically accessible data and the domain of hypothetical parameters underlying the data.

These characteristics of Bayesian models in statistics are generally shared by Bayesian models in perceptual and cognitive psychology. They imply that the construction or application of a Bayesian model most often takes the form of a complex net of probability constructs, which are linked in a definite precedence order and may differ in the roles assigned to the same variables involved. Standard terms of the Bayesian lexicon such as “prior distribution”, “likelihood function”, and “posterior distribution” are used for orientation within the complex net of entities and relations implicit in a Bayesian structure. However, the real complexity of a model may well exceed what is suggested by these three standard terms, because the model may involve multiple priors or likelihoods or posteriors, and the distribution serving as the prior at one stage of the implied Bayesian processing may be the posterior of a previous stage within the same processing.

In this article, we take a general view of the relations implicit in Bayesian models and explore their properties in algebraic terms. Specifically, we characterize the probabilistic entities generally involved in those relations, define the basic operations to which those entities are subjected, illustrate the formal properties of such operations, and highlight the algebraic character of the system of entities and operations. There are some benefits that one may expect from this analysis, such as a method for keeping track of the sequence of moves in the construction and application of a Bayesian model, a guide for recognizing the assumptions required for the legitimacy of those moves, and a common platform for comparing Bayesian models in their formal organization.

The entities (called “probability kernels”), a set of three basic operations on them, and the formal properties of these operations are specified in Section  2 . The algebraic form of the system (a lattice) is made explicit in the next two sections, by first considering only variables suitably combined (Section  3 ) and then discussing probability kernels acting on the variables (Section  4 ). In Section  5  we refer to conditional stochastic independence, which often enters as a postulate in Bayesian models, and indicate its place within the suggested algebraic framework. In Section  6  we illustrate the use of the framework by applying it as a method for representing and discussing Bayesian models from perceptual and cognitive psychology.

Our use of the word “kernel” is consistent with the meaning associated with this word in the theories of Markov processes, of graphical statistical models, and more generally of conditionally specified probabilistic models; that is, a kernel  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Y 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       as a family of probability distributions on the space of a variable  
                         Y 
                       that are conditional on the values of a variable  
                         X 
                      
 ( Lauritzen, 1996 , p. 46;  Meyn & Tweedie, 1993 , p. 65). 
                         1 
                       
                      
                         1 
                         The “kernel” concept, in its full extent, can be formulated in measure-theoretic terms. Specifically, a kernel  
                               
                                  p 
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     Y 
                                     | 
                                     X 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                             is tantamount to a family (indexed by the values of variable  
                               X 
                            ) of probability measures on the space  
                               
                                  
                                     Y 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∘ 
                                  
                               
                             (this space being endowed with a definite  
                               σ 
                            -field) that are absolutely continuous with respect to some fixed reference measure on  
                               
                                  
                                     Y 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∘ 
                                  
                               
                            , so that they are representable as density functions (Radon–Nikodym derivatives) having  
                               
                                  
                                     Y 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∘ 
                                  
                               
                             as their domain ( Billingsley, 1995  pp. 439–440). However, for the specific aims of our study, this general formulation of the concept is not required. We may limit ourselves to referring to kernels in which the probability measures are either all of discrete type (i.e., absolutely continuous with respect to a suitable counting measure) or all of continuous type (i.e., absolutely continuous with respect to a suitable Lebesgue measure), as stipulated in  Definition 1 . A measure-theoretic framing of the concept would be especially beneficial in enhancing the generality of our arguments — for example, overcoming the limitations of the promotion operation as specified in  Definition 2 .

This definition collects the basic operations we need for constructing our algebra of probability kernels. In their meanings, and in part in their suggested names, they correspond to operations ordinarily performed on conditional probabilities in Bayesian computations ( Bernardo & Smith, 2000 , pp. 127–130;  Koski & Noble, 2009 , pp. 53–57). In symbolic terms, a projection acted upon a kernel  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Y 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       has the effect of cancelling a sub-variable of  
                         Y 
                      , a conditioning displaces a sub-variable of  
                         Y 
                       to the right of the bar (i.e., a conditioned variable becomes conditioning), whereas a promotion displaces a sub-variable of  
                         X 
                       to the left of the bar (i.e., a conditioning variable becomes conditioned). Note that conditioning and promotion are somehow converse of each other: in a conditioning we start with a kernel  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Z 
                               ∪ 
                               W 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , derive  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               W 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       from it through projection (Eq.  (1)  or  (2)  in suitable rewrite), and then obtain  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Z 
                               | 
                               W 
                               ∪ 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       by division according to Eq.  (3) , whereas in a promotion we start with two kernels  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Z 
                               | 
                               W 
                               ∪ 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               W 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and then obtain  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Z 
                               ∪ 
                               W 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       by multiplication according to Eq.  (4)  in suitable rewrite. Also note that the promotion operation is here defined  only  with reference to any two kernels  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               Y 
                               | 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               V 
                               | 
                               U 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       (satisfying condition  
                         
                            X 
                            = 
                            V 
                            ∪ 
                            U 
                         
                      ) that are “of the same type”, that is, the two kernels are either both discrete or both continuous, as understood in  Definition 1 . Of course, this is a limitation in the use of the operation, which however is not prejudicial to the specific aims of our present study. A wider definition, which would allow combining kernels of different types through promotion, should be framed using concepts from measure theory (see footnote 1).

About the three basic operations on kernels, we add the following comments. First, by referring to the results of the stated operations as “kernels” we take for granted that they are actually families of probability functions, in compliance with  Definition 1 . The proofs for all three operations are straightforward. In particular, Eq.  (3)  indicates that, in order that the result

As concerns promotion  
                         M 
                      , we follow the rule of symbolizing its result

                               V

                               U

                            X 
                            = 
                            V 
                            ∪ 
                            U 
                         
                      . Of course, if a kernel is a promoter of another kernel, then the latter cannot be a promoter of the former, and for this reason promotion fails to be a commutative operation. It is, however, an associative operation.

The remainder of this section is devoted to illustrating the expressive power of the basic operations on kernels — that is, how, by suitably combining these operations, we may express important kinds of moves in Bayesian computations. We focus on two general forms, which combine conditioning  
                         C 
                       or projection  
                         J 
                       with promotion  
                         M 
                      . The first is given by this formula  
                         
                            (11)

                      which has to be interpreted as a production rule, not as an equation requiring a proof. It shows that, if we are given a kernel

                               ( 
                               Y 
                               ∪ 
                               Z 
                               | 
                               V 
                               ∪

                               V

As shown by the arguments developed so far, an important role in determining the properties of probability kernels is played by the variable-pairs on which the kernels are acting. As a convenient entry to the algebra of kernels, in this section we will study the set of such variable-pairs. Given a full variable

Using the labels in  Fig. 1 , the equations that specify joins and meets in the lattice

                                     = 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        A 
                                        ∪ 
                                        B 
                                        ∪ 
                                        C 
                                        ∪ 
                                        D 
                                        ∪ 
                                        E

                                        F 
                                        ∪ 
                                        G

                                        H

                                     0̸ 
                                      and

                                     0̸

                       The atoms in the lattice are the pairs  
                         
                            ( 
                            V 
                            | 
                            U 
                            ) 
                         
                       such that  
                         
                            | 
                            V 
                            | 
                            = 
                            1 
                         
                      , that is, the left component is an elementary variable. Thus, if  
                         
                            n 
                            = 
                            | 
                            T 
                            | 
                         
                      , then there are  
                         
                            n 
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  n 
                                  − 
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         
                       atoms. It is readily proved that any member of  
                         
                            O 
                            
                               ( 
                               T 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is expressible as the join of suitably chosen atoms and that the lattice is rankable, the rank of any pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            V 
                            | 
                            U 
                            ) 
                         
                       being the cardinality  
                         
                            | 
                            V 
                            | 
                         
                       of its left component. It can also be proved that the lattice is locally distributive. Specifically, join and meet are distributive whenever applied to members whose pairwise meets are all different from  
                         ⊥ 
                      . The lattice is not fully distributive. For example, if  
                         U 
                      ,  
                         V 
                      , and  
                         W 
                       are non-empty disjoint variables, then  
                         
                            ( 
                            V 
                            | 
                            U 
                            ) 
                         
                      ,  
                         
                            (

The stated definition is theoretically important but of little practical use, because of the existential quantification “there is a

                               (

The next proposition provides such a condition, which deserves consideration also because it only depends on the set-theoretic configuration of the variable-pairs in the kernels, not on the form of the probability functions constituting those kernels.

Note that this corollary corresponds to a key result in the theory of Bayesian networks. In fact, if a set of conditional independences is representable in terms of an  acyclic  directed graph, then a list of variable-pairs with the stated characteristics does exist, with

Therefore, a probability distribution on the whole set of variables in the network becomes uniquely determined on the basis of the assumed independences and by combining conditional distributions on individual variables through the “chain rule”, which corresponds to multiple promotion in our terms ( Kjærulff & Madsen, 2008 , pp. 58–60;  Pearl, 1988 , pp. 119–120).

                       within a lattice of probability kernels.

Bayesian models generally include assumptions of stochastic independence between some of the variables they involve, in addition to assumptions concerning the type of probability distributions of those variables. Typically, those are assumptions of  conditional  independence that state that two (or more) variables are independent of one another when a certain other variable is fixed at any of its possible values. In this section we mention basic concepts about conditional independence, in order to indicate the place they hold within the algebraic framework developed in the previous sections. Furthermore, these concepts are required for describing the examples in the next section.

This observation, combined with formula  (23) , leads us to the following conclusion as concerns the place of stochastic independence within a lattice of kernels. Any postulate of multiple independence in a Bayesian model implies a set of binary independences, and each of these is expressible as an interchangeability link between two kernels. Therefore, the independence postulate may be represented as a net of interchangeability links between members of the lattice

                               T

In this section, we illustrate how the algebraic framework we have designed may be used for representing and analyzing the structure of Bayesian models that are created in psychological science. We choose for discussion two examples, one from perceptual psychology that concerns spatial vision, and the other from cognitive psychology that concerns category representation.

In presenting the examples, we make use of pictures that are consistent with the Hasse diagrams in  Fig. 2 , except that, for convenience of notation, they are constructed as two-dimensional rather than three-dimensional graphical structures. Resorting to graphical tools is a common practice with methods belonging to the area of Bayesian theories. This is illustrated, for example, by the flow charts representing graphical models and hierarchical Bayesian models in statistics and in psychological studies ( Bartlema, Lee, Wetzels, & Vanpaemel, 2014 ;  Jordan, 2004 ;  Lee, 2008 ;  Lunn et al., 2013 , chapter 10) and by the directed acyclic graphs (DAGs) that encode dependence and independence relations in Bayesian networks ( Kjærulff & Madsen, 2008 , chapter 2;  Pearl, 1988 , chapter 3). Note, however, two peculiarities of our diagrams. One is that in these diagrams the nodes represent probability kernels (not variables), and the lines stand for operations on kernels — projection, conditioning, and promotion operations, as specified by  Definition 2 . The other is that for the construction of our diagrams we rely on a general theoretical basis, which is the lattice structure of any complete set of probability kernels or variable-pairs, as certified by  Proposition 4 .

The first example we discuss is a model for how the perceived slant of a surface depends jointly on two specific stimulus properties, named “figural compression cue” and “stereo cue”. The model is described and experimentally tested in  Knill (2007a) , from which we take the names and symbols, and is also applied in  Knill (2007b)  and  Seydell, Knill, and Trommershäuser (2010) . It involves the following main variables:

                                   is the “slant of surfaces in the world” 
                                  α 
                                   is the “aspect ratio of ellipses in the world”

                                  c

This observation also applies to the other example in the current section.

The whole system of primitive, intermediate, and final kernels resulting from our parsing of  Knill ’s ( 2007a ) model is represented in  Fig. 3 . Consistent with the Hasse diagrams in  Fig. 2 , in this representation we classify the kernels according to the number of variables involved in them (increasing from left to right) and the number of conditioned variables (increasing from bottom to top). The seven primitive kernels are in boldface and the final kernel is underlined. Furthermore, ascending solid and dashed lines represent applications of promotion and of multiplication (because of independence), respectively, whereas descending solid and dashed lines represent applications of projection and of conditioning, respectively.

We conclude this example with comments on properties that are visible from the given representation. First, all primitive kernels in the model are atoms in the associated lattice of kernels, as each of them involves one single left variable. This characteristic agrees with the observation we made when introducing  Definition 4 , concerning how Bayesian models are actually constructed. The second example we consider is a Bayesian model of category representation. It has been proposed as a refinement in hierarchical Bayesian form of the Varying Abstraction Model (VAM), which in turn has been developed as a paradigm capable of encompassing the exemplar and prototype accounts of category representation and learning ( Vanpaemel & Storms, 2008, 2010 ). Details and applications of the model are presented in  Lee and Vanpaemel (2008)  and  Vanpaemel (2011) . In our discussion, we will refer to the comparatively simple experimental situation hypothesized in Sections  2 and 3  of the paper by  Lee and Vanpaemel (2008) . On the model thus illustrated comments may be made that are similar to those given for the preceding example — such as the minimal rank of all primitive kernels in the system, and the independence assumptions required for some crucial operations in the system. To these we add two particular remarks. One is about the distribution  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               X 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , which on one side is the prior on the target psychological variable  
                         X 
                      , and on the other side is the output of a sub-process driven by  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               θ 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      ,  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               γ 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , and  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               X 
                               | 
                               θ 
                               , 
                               γ 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      . This is a simple example of the duplicity or flexibility of roles in Bayesian constructions. The other remark is about the kernel  
                         
                            p 
                            
                               ( 
                               X 
                               | 
                               θ 
                               , 
                               γ 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , which is a family of probability distributions not defined by analytic equations but constructed (in approximate form) through computations that simulate a random experiment. This is an example of probability kernels that enter into Bayesian modeling when the variables of concern are of a combinatorial, rather than of a standard numerical, type ( Gershman & Blei, 2012 ).

References to Bayes rule and the associated concepts of prior distribution, likelihood function, and posterior distribution, form the core of the language currently used in explaining Bayesian models of psychological phenomena. However, when examined in detail, several Bayesian models actually set up in psychology reveal quite a complex organization that is only partly captured by those standard concepts. In the construction of a Bayesian model, and in fitting it to experimental data, complex chains of transformations or combinations of probabilistic constructs may take place, with the possible cancellation, reappearance, or exchange of roles of the variables involved. A systematic framework is desirable for representing and analyzing the nets of operations that Bayesian models in psychology may imply.

We addressed this problem by referring to a comprehensive class of probability constructs – probability kernels as understood in  Definition 1  – and to three basic operations on them — projection, conditioning, and promotion, as specified in  Definition 2 . We then showed that other important operations in Bayesian constructions, such as Bayes rule itself and the so-called marginalization of likelihoods, can be expressed as compositions of these basic operations. We also showed that a suitable combination of the basic operations gives rise to a binary relation upon any complete set of kernels, which endows that set with a regular algebraic structure – the structure of a lattice – so that further operations, join and meet, come to be a part of the system. The fact that this system allows for a consistent placement of theoretically important concepts, such as the induction of probability distributions, the compatibility relation between conditional distributions, and the relation of stochastic independence, may be viewed as further evidence of the fitness of our proposal.

There are benefits that may be expected from having a method for parsing complex Bayesian models, such as: producing detailed descriptions of the chains of operations implied by those models; recognizing distinct roles, such as being an input, intermediate, or output probability kernel, in those chains; highlighting assumptions that are required for the legitimacy of any single operation; and verifying whether the architecture of a model may be simplified without changing its empirical purpose. The evidence we have collected by applying our framework to the examples in Section  6  and other examples from the psychological literature suggests that the algebraic approach we adopted is a promising way to achieve these aims.
